padding-left: 0.23em;">+</span><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-32" style="padding-left: 0.23em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.139em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1000.68em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-33"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-34" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">O</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.741em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-35"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-36" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">2</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-37" style="padding-left: 0.344em;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-38" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">→</span><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-39" style="padding-left: 0.344em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-40"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-41" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">3</span><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-42"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.219em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1001.76em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-43"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-44" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">SnO</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 1.764em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-45"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-46" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">2</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-47" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.23em;">+</span><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-48" style="padding-left: 0.23em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.594em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1001.14em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-49"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-50" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">Co</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 1.196em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-51"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-52" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">3</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-53"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.139em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1000.68em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-54"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-55" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">O</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.741em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-56"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-57" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">4</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.219em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.066em;"></span></span></nobr><span class="MJX_Assistive_MathML MJX_Assistive_MathML_Block" role="presentation"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>3</mn><mi>SnCo</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>3</mn><msub><mrow><mi>SnO</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>Co</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>3</mn><mi>SnCo</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>3</mn><msub><mrow><mi>SnO</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>Co</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>The carbonization degree of B-SnCo/NCFs was characterized by the Raman spectrum, the results of which are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. The two broad peaks located at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be attributed to the D band (structural defects in the lattice of carbon atoms) and G band (in-plane stretching vibration of the sp<sup>2</sup> hybridization of carbon atoms). It is commonly believed that the intensity ratio of the D band and the G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) can be used to illustrate the graphitization trait of the composite materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> for B-SnCo/NCFs is 1.37, proving that the B-SnCo/NCFs have some defects in carbonaceous material resulting from the introduction of HCSs into N-doped carbon nanofibers, while the SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs are 1.18, 1.20, and 1.08. The introduction of defects can increase the storage of the Na ion because of the binding energy between Na ions, and the defects can enhance the Na adsorption on the surface of carbon materials. Moreover, it can provide fast electron transport during the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process, contributing to the outstanding electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 32 to 34">(32−34)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/asset/images/medium/ic3c00646_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/asset/images/large/ic3c00646_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) TGA curve of B-SnCo/NCFs; (b) Raman pattern of B-SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs; (c) BET curves of B-SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs; (d) conductivity pattern of B-SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs; XPS spectra of (e) survey spectrum, (f) C 1s, (g) N 1s, (h) Co 2p, and (i) Sn 3d of B-SnCo/NCFs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/asset/images/large/ic3c00646_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method and Barrett–Joyner–Halenda method were used to calculate the specific surface area and pore size distribution of four samples. The specific surface area of B-SnCo/NCFs is 109.06 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, while the specific area is 97.90 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for B-Sn/NCFs, 88.37 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for SnCo/NCFs, and 91.15 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for Sn/NCFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). The B-SnCo/NCFs with a large specific surface area can provide not only enough contact area between the electrolyte and active materials but also abundant void space for the volume variation of SnCo during the charge and discharge process. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> shows the pore size distribution of B-SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs. It is clearly observed that the B-SnCo/NCFs have a mesoporous structure with a pore size of 4 nm. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d displays the electrical conductivities of B-SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs. It can be seen that B-SnCo/NCFs have the highest electrical conductivity (3.56 S m<sup>–1</sup>). The excellent conductivity of B-SnCo/NCFs can be attributed to the synergistic effect of hollow mesoporous carbon spheres and good conductivity of carbon fibers.</div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out to examine the compositions and the surface chemical states of B-SnCo/NCFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e–i). The full spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e reveals the presence of C, Co, Sn, and N elements in B-SnCo/NCFs, which proves the successful synthesis of these elements and coincides with the EDS analysis. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f shows the high-resolution spectrum of C 1s, in which the three peaks located at 283.7, 284.3, and 287.3 eV can be attributed to the C═C, C═N, and C–N bonds, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The N 1s spectrum of B-SnCo/NCFs can be fitted to the three peaks at 397.3, 399.0, and 399.6 eV, which can be ascribed to to pyridinic, pyrrolic, and graphitic N, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g). It should be noted that nitrogen derives from the decomposition and carbonization of PAN. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Based on the XPS and TEM-EDS results, the N content in the B-SnCo/NCFs composite is ∼10%. The introduction of N can improve the conductivity and provide more active sites for sodium ions. The Co 2p XPS spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h) of B-SnCo/NCFs can be divided into 785.0, 780.4, and 777.2 eV, which can be related to the Co<sup>3+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>0</sup>, respectively, and the two additional peaks at 796.0 and 801.6 correspond to Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> As for the Sn 3d (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i), the peaks located at 485.7 and 494.0 eV belong to the Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Sn<sup>4+</sup>, and the other two peaks at 484.0 and 492.4 eV can be attributed to the metallic Sn, confirming the existence of SnCo in the nanocomposite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of B-SnCo/NCFs and the three reference samples were further studied by CR2025-type coin half cells. The B-SnCo/NCF films were directly used as the anode without binder and conductive material. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a shows the initial three cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves of the B-SnCo/NCF anode with a voltage window of 0.005 and 3.0 V at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The irreversible broad peaks at 0.4 and 1.2 V correspond to the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> The subsequent peaks between 0.25 and 0.005 V can be indexed to the multiple step alloying reactions with Sn, loading in Na<sub>2.25</sub>Sn, Na<sub>3</sub>Sn, and Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> During the corresponding anodic scan, the peaks at 0.59 and 0.75 V can be attributed to the dealloying reaction of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn<i><sub>y</sub></i>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Commonly, the alloying and dealloying reactions of SnCo can be understood based on the following equations <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" style="text-align: left;"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0" data-mathml="<math xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML&quot; padding-left: 0.23em;">+</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-109" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.23em;">3.75</span><span class="msup" id="MathJax-Span-110"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.707em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1001.14em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-111"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-112" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">Na</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.372em; left: 1.196em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-113"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-114" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">+</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-115" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; position: relative; width: 1.026em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.355em, 1000.4em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-118"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-119" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">e</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.372em; left: 0.457em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-120"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-121" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">−</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.219em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.191em;"></span></span></nobr><span class="MJX_Assistive_MathML MJX_Assistive_MathML_Block" role="presentation"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3.75</mn></mrow></msub><mi>Sn</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">↔</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>Sn</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>3.75</mn><msup><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><mn>3.75</mn><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-4"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3.75</mn></mrow></msub><mi>Sn</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">↔</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>Sn</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>3.75</mn><msup><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><mn>3.75</mn><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div>Notably, the curves of the B-SnCo/NCFs in the following cathodic scan and anodic scan tend to overlap, indicating the splendid reversibility of the B-SnCo/NCF anode during the cycling. For comparison, the CV curves of B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a–c</a>) are presented in the same conditions, which implies that the same electrochemical reactions occurred.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/asset/images/medium/ic3c00646_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/asset/images/large/ic3c00646_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV profiles of B-SnCo/NCFs at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and (b) charge/discharge curves of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode; (c) rate performance of B-SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs; (d) cycle performance of B-SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs; (e) EIS pattern and (f) the fitted lines between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode; (g) CV curves at different scan rates; (h) fitted lines between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i> of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/asset/images/large/ic3c00646_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b exhibits the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of B-SnCo/NCFs for the initial three cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The platforms at different discharge and charge voltage stages are in accordance with the oxidation and reduction peaks in CV curves. The discharge and charge capacities of B-SnCo/NCFs for the first cycle are 850.5 and 495.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 58.2%. The initial capacity loss is mainly due to the formation of the SEI film and the irreversible reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> However, the SnCo/NCF, B-Sn/NCF, and Sn/NCF electrodes deliver much lower charge–discharge capacities of 802.4 and 415.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 685.6 and 330 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, and 807.8 and 477.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7d–f</a>). The B-SnCo/NCF anode displays a better ICE value and a higher specific capacity than the three reference samples, which can be attributed to the introduction of HCS and the active material SnCo alloy. The rate performance of B-SnCo/NCFs and the three reference samples was further investigated under a series of current densities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). The B-SnCo/NCF anode presents excellent reversible capacities of 463.1, 396.7, 346.4, 301.6, and 243.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, and 1.6 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific discharge capacity of B-SnCo/NCFs could still remain at 379.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, the three reference samples show lower reversible capacities than the B-SnCo/NCF electrode. This proves that the addition of HCS further alleviates the severe volume expansion in the charge–discharge process and agglomeration of the SnCo metal, and the Co used as volume buffers does not participate in the reaction of alloying/dealloying, thus enhancing the electrochemical stability of Sn-based alloys. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> On the other hand, the addition of Co can also improve the conductivity of the material, promote the diffusion and transfer of electrons/ions, and improve the rate capability of B-SnCo/NCFs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> To evaluate the influence of HCS and Co on the electrochemical properties of SnCo alloys, the cyclic performances of B-SnCo/NCFs, SnCo/NCFs, B-Sn/NCFs, and Sn/NCFs were tested at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> current density, the results of which are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d. To be specific, the initial discharge specific capacities of the B-SnCo/NCF, SnCo/NCF, B-Sn/NCF, and Sn/NCF anodes are 827, 820, 705, and 700 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. After 300 cycles, the reversible specific capacity is 351 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. However, SnCo/NCF composites attenuate rapidly, and the specific capacity after cycling is 243.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which indicates that HCSs can effectively slow down the volume expansion of the alloy. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> shows the cycling performances of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>; the discharge specific capacity of the B-SnCo/NCF anode is stabilized at 198 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles. Compared with other Sn-based electrodes in related literature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, Supporting Information), the B-SnCo/NCF anode still has great advantages in terms of reversible capacity and cycle stability. In addition, we also synthesized SnCo/NCF samples with an SnCo alloy content of 17.4% and different Sn and Co ratios (2:1, 3:1) used as anodes for SIBs, and the results are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a,b</a>, respectively. It can be observed that the SnCo/NCFs with a Sn and Co ratio of 1:1 and an SnCo alloy content of 34.8% used as anodes have the best electrochemical performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further verify the excellent performance of B-SnCo/NCF electrodes, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) spectra of B-SnCo/NCF and SnCo/NCF electrodes before and after 3 cycles of measurements were explored to analyze the sodium-ion transport kinetics. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10a</a>, all Nyquist diagrams are composed of a semicircle in the high-frequency region and a slant line in the low-frequency region. In the equivalent circuit model (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e), the semicircle in the high-frequency region reflects the SEI film resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at the electrode electrolyte interface. The linear slope in the low-frequency region represents the diffusion resistance of sodium ions, namely Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>). Obviously, the B-SnCo/NCF electrode has a smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> than the SnCo/NCF anode under the fresh and post-three-cycles conditions, indicating that the unique bean pod-like structure can accelerate the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> storage and maintain the integrity of the structure. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> B-SnCo/NCF electrode is decreased after three cycles, which is owing to the increased wettability of the integrated carbon substrate and electrolyte. By fitting the <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> diagram to analyze the diffusion process of Na<sup>+</sup>, the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> can be calculated by the following formula <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><span id="MathJax-Element-6"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msup><mrow><mi>Z</mi></mrow><mrow><mo class="MJX-variant" mathsize="8pt">′</mo></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div>where <i>R</i> and <i>F</i> are gas constants and Faraday constants, respectively, <i>A</i> is the specific surface area of the electrode, σ<sub>w</sub> represents Warburg coefficient, and ω represents the angular frequency. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f, the slope of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode is 382.8 and that of the SnCo/NCF electrode is 261.5 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a>) under fresh condition. After 3 cycles, the slope of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode is 382.8 and the slope of the SnCo/NCF electrode is 402.9, which further indicates that the introduction of HCSs can prevent the self-aggregation of SnCo nanoparticles, shorten the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channel, and accelerate the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to analyze the transport kinetics of B-SnCo/NCFs and the three reference electrodes, the CV performance of the electrode was tested at a scanning speed of 0.2–1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The test results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>g and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a–c, respectively. The dynamic relationship between current (<i>i</i>) and scanning rate (<i>v</i>) satisfies the following equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a–c) CV curves at different scan rates of SnCo/NCF, B-Sn/CNF, and Sn/CNF electrodes; (d–f) fitted lines between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i> of SnCo/NCF, B-Sn/CNF, and Sn/CNF anodes; (g) capacitive-controlled contribution at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (h) ratio of the capacitive contributions of B-SnCo/NCF, SnCo/NCF, B-Sn/CNF, and Sn/CNF electrodes, and the GITT diagrams of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode; (i) sodium diffusion coefficient diagrams of the B-SnCo/NCF anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/asset/images/large/ic3c00646_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">According to the reported literature, the <i>b</i>-value of 0.5 denotes diffusion-controlled behavior, while the <i>b</i>-value of 1.0 represents the capacitive behavior. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h, the calculated <i>b</i>-values of the two peaks were 0.91 and 0.73, respectively, indicating that both diffusion-controlled and capacitive-controlled behaviors are present in the B-SnCo/NCF electrode. For comparison, in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d–f, the <i>b</i>-values of the SnCo/NCFs are 0.81 and 0.80, those of B-Sn/NCFs are 0.76 and 0.51, and those of Sn/NCFs are 0.78 and 0.75, respectively. The <i>b</i>-value of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode was higher than that of the three reference samples. indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-10"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>V</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>/</mo><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(10)</span></div></div>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> denotes diffusion control and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i> denotes capacitive control. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>g shows the contribution of capacitance behavior to B-SnCo/NCFs at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. It can be seen that the capacitive contribution ratio of B-SnCo/NCFs is 60.6% at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Theoretically, the pseudocapacitance should be less than the actual current. However, our calculated method may not be accurate, resulting in a small part exceeding the actual current. On the other hand, it may be caused by the polarization of the electrode. This phenomenon was also found to exist in other works we have published. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> In contrast, the capacitance contribution of SnCo/NCF, B-Sn/NCF, and Sn/NCF anodes was about 56.4, 52.8, and 49.2%, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a–c</a>). With the scanning rate increasing, the capacitive contribution also increased. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>h shows the capacitive contribution ratio at different scanning rates of B-SnCo/NCF, SnCo/NCF, B-Sn/NCF, and Sn/NCF anodes. When the current density increases to 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the pseudocapacitance ratios of B-SnCo/NCFs are 64.4, 70.9, 75.8, and 81.6%, respectively. It can be obviously seen that the capacitance contribution of the B-SnCo/NCFs was the highest at different scanning rates than the three reference samples, which, owing to the unique bean pod-like structure, can provide space for the volume expansion and accelerate the transmission of Na<sup>+</sup>. Meanwhile, the SnCo nanoparticles shorten the diffusion path of Na<sup>+</sup> to improve the electrochemical performance of B-SnCo/NCFs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical kinetic behaviors of B-SnCo/NCF and SnCo/NCF electrodes were investigated by the constant-current intermittent titration (GITT) technique to evaluate the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>), which gave a constant current density of 100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.005–3.0 V. mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow><mrow><mi>τ</mi><mi>π</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>m</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi>V</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>M</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>τ</mi></mrow></msub></mrow></mfrac><mo constant current, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> and <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> represent the mass and the molar mass of active materials, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> and <i>S</i> are the molar volume active material and the area of the electrode, respectively, Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the change in the steady-state potential after a discharge pulse and open-circuit standing, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> represents the voltage change during a discharge pulse. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10c,d</a> shows the GITT diagrams of discharge and the charge of B-SnCo/NCF and SnCo/NCF anodes, which are consistent with the discharge and charge curves. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>i, the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode is between 10<sup>–12.4</sup> and 10<sup>–13.7</sup> during the discharge process, while the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode is between 10<sup>–11.9</sup> and 10<sup>–12.6</sup> in the charge process. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10e</a> displays the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of the SnCo/NCF electrode during the discharge and charge process. By comparison, it can be seen that the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value of the B-SnCo/NCF electrode is much higher than that of the SnCo/NCF anode, proving that HCSs can provide sufficient diffusion channels for Na<sup>+</sup>, which are consistent with the EIS results.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i19">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have successfully obtained the bean pea-like freestanding flexible B-SnCo/NCFs membrane. The unique bean pod-like structure can provide space to alleviate the volume change of the SnCo alloy. This synthesis method can be directly used to obtain a freestanding membrane as the electrode material to avoid the power failure and crack caused by binder and conductive agents, and can improve the energy density of the material. As the anode for SIBs, the B-SnCo/NCF membrane reveals an ultrastable specific capacity of 351 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles. Due to the simplicity of the synthesis method, this flexible freestanding membrane electrode is conducive to large-scale commercial applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i20">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM and TEM images of HCSs; XRD pattern and SEM; HRTEM and SAED images of reference samples; pore size distribution; CV curves; the charge/discharge curves; the cycling performances of B-SnCo/NCFs anode at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>; comparisons between this work and reported works in terms of rate capability and cycling performance; EIS and GITT data; pseudocapacitive contribution (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646/suppl_file/ic3c00646_si_001.pdf">ic3c00646_si_001.pdf (848.37 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00646" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19-29</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202112072"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dual-redox sites guarantee high-capacity sodium storage in two-dimension conjugated metal–organic frameworks</span>. <i>Adv. Funct. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>32</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">2112072</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.202112072</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D32%26publication_year%3D2022%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DB.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DM.%2BYe%26author%3DY.%2BZhang%26author%3DY.%2BTang%26author%3DX.%2BHu%26author%3DJ.%2BHe%26author%3DC.%2BLi%26title%3DDual-redox%2Bsites%2Bguarantee%2Bhigh-capacity%2Bsodium%2Bstorage%2Bin%2Btwo-dimension%2Bconjugated%2Bmetal%25E2%2580%2593organic%2Bframeworks%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.202112072&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.inorgchem.3c00646&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.202112072&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Dual-Redox Sites Guarantee High-Capacity Sodium Storage in Two-Dimension Conjugated Metal-Organic Frameworks</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Bo; Li, Jianming; Ye, Minghui; Zhang, Yufei; Tang, Yongchao; Hu, Xuanhe; He, Jun; Li, Cheng Chao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2112072</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">2D π-d conjugated metal-org. frameworks (c-MOFs) are promising anode candidates for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high intrinsic cond. and stability in org. electrolytes.  However, the development of c-MOFs with multi-redox sites to improve the overall performance of SIBs is highly desired but remains a great challenge.  Herein, this work reports the electrochem. active hexaazatrinaphthylene-based 2D π-d c-MOFs (HATN-XCu, X = O or S) as advanced anode materials with dual-redox sites for SIBs.  The ordered porous and layer-stacked structure can provide fast transmission and diffusion channels for ions along the stacking directions.  Ex situ Fourier transformed IR spectra together with XPS reveal the dual-redox site storage mechanism of HATN-XCu, namely, the continuous multi-electron reactions occurring on the redox-active CN group and [CuX4] unit, resp.  Based on the synergistic effect of dual-redox sites, HATN-OCu anode exhibits impressive reversible capacity (500 mAh g-1 at 0.1 A g-1) and high-rate performance (151 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1).  Significantly, a sodium-ion full battery assembled using a HATN-OCu anode and Na3V2(PO4)2O2F cathode also displays high-rate performance (117 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1) and stable long-cycle life (the capacity retention of 80% after 500 cycles at 2 A g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201800212"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exploration of advanced electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Evolution of the electrochemical interface in sodium ion batteries with ether electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Kaikai; Zhang, Jun; Lin, Dongmei; Wang, Da-Wei; Li, Baohua; Lv, Wei; Sun, Sheng; He, Yan-Bing; Kang, Feiyu; Yang, Quan-Hong; Zhou, Limin; Zhang, Tong-Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ether based electrolytes have surfaced as alternatives to conventional carbonates allowing for enhanced electrochem. performance of sodium-ion batteries; however, the primary source of the improvement remains poorly understood.  Here we show that coupling titanium dioxide and other anode materials with diglyme does enable higher efficiency and reversible capacity than those for the combination involving ester electrolytes.  Importantly, the electrolyte dependent performance is revealed to be the result of the different structural evolution induced by a varied sodiation depth.  A suit of characterizations show that the energy barrier to charge transfer at the interface between electrolyte and electrode is the factor that dominates the interfacial electrochem. characteristics and therefore the energy storage properties.  Our study proposes a reliable parameter to assess the intricate sodiation dynamics in sodium-ion batteries and could guide the design of aprotic electrolytes for next generation rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2021.04.009"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tomboc, G.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Promises and Challenges of Sn-Based Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Chenjing; Hou, Xiaoxiao; Bai, Zhongchao; Yun, Longteng; Zhang, Xuanrui; Wang, Nana; Yang, Jian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chinese Journal of Chemistry</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn-based composites are considered as one of the promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high theor. capacities and suitable operation voltages.  However, they suffer the huge vol. change and unstable electrolyte/electrode interface during sodiation/desodiation, which causes structure collapse and serious capacity loss.  In the past few years, great efforts have been made to ameliorate the performances of Sn by rationally designing electrode structures/components or optimizing electrolyte compn.  Here, the underlying sodium-storage reactions in Sn are summarized based on the results from theor. calcns. and advanced characterizations.  Then, the recent progresses of Sn-based composites, i.e., Sn-based alloys, Sn/C composites, and Sn oxides/sulfides, with various structures for SIBs are discussed in details.  After that, how the electrolyte affects solid-electrolyte interphase and the electrochem. performances is shown.  Finally, the future directions for the applications of Sn-based composites in SIBs are suggested.  Hence, this review provides a comprehensive scenario about the applications of Sn-based composites in SIBs.  It will benefit the future development of alloy-type anodes in SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1038/s41578-021-00324-w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Usiskin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Popovic, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Law, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balaya, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maier, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fundamentals, status and promise of sodium-based batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Bimetallic alloy SbSn nanodots filled in electrospun N-doped carbon fibers for high performance Na-ion battery anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Lijie; Yang, Bin; Chen, Xin; Wang, Hui; Dang, Jie; Liu, Xiaojie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">389</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">138246</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In the current state of research, it is still a challenge to develop high-performance electrode materials with high energy d. and long cycle life for Na-ion batteries.  For example, Owing to the serious vol. changes of the anode active materials in the actual application of the Na-ion batteries, which resulting in irreversible capacity loss and poor cycle stability of the battery.  Taking advantage of different redox potentials of metals proved to be an effective method to reduce vol. changes.  Here SbSn nanodots are encapsulated in N-doped C nanofibers prepd. by electrospinning.  The different oxidn. depotentialities of Sn and Sb in SbSn helps to reduce the vol. expansion during the cycle, three-dimensional interconnected N-doped C nanofibers(NCNFs) have a high sp. surface area and high porosity, which are conducive to the transmission of electrons and ions.  The resulting SbSn nanodots filled in N-doped C nanofibers(SbSn@NCNFs) composite exhibits impressive electrochem. performance, with high discharge capacity (808mAh g-1at 100mAg-1), rate capability(331mAh g-1 at 1.6Ag-1) and excellent cycle stability (331mAh g-1 at 100mAg-1 after 500 cycles). To address this issue, amorphous bimetallic active-inactive nanoalloy Co-Sn with Sn acting as a high capacity active compd. and Co acting as a conductive inactive matrix has been explored here.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we provide the first report on several compns. of ternary Sn-Ge-Sb thin film alloys for application as sodium ion battery (aka NIB, NaB or SIB) anodes, employing Sn50Ge50, Sb50Ge50, and pure Sn, Ge, Sb as baselines. This alloy has an initial reversible specific capacity of 833 mAhg-1 (at 85 mAg-1) and 662 mAhg-1 after 50 charge-discharge cycles.  Sn50Ge25Sb25 also shows excellent rate capability, displaying a stable capacity of 381 mAhg-1 at a c.d. of 8500 mAg-1 (∼10C).  A survey of published literature indicates that 833 mAhg-1 is among the highest reversible capacities reported for a Sn-based NIB anode, while 381 mAhg-1 represents the optimum fast charge value.  HRTEM shows that Sn50Ge25Sb25 is a composite of 10-15 nm Sn and Sn-alloyed Ge nanocrystallites that are densely dispersed within an amorphous matrix.  Comparing the microstructures of alloys where the capacity significantly exceeds the rule of mixts. prediction to those where it does not leads us to hypothesize that this new phenomenon originates from the Ge(Sn) that is able to sodiate beyond the 1:1 Na:Ge ratio reported for the pure element.  Combined TOF-SIMS, EELS TEM, and FIB anal. demonstrates substantial Na segregation within the film near the current collector interface that is present as early as the second discharge, followed by cycling-induced delamination from the current collector.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1021/nl5028606"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van Aken, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maier, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile synthesis of highly porous Ni–Sn intermetallic microcages with excellent electrochemical performance for lithium and sodium storage</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">6387</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6392</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Most literature reports show that their performance decreases significantly caused by oxidation in alkaline electrolyte during electrochemical testing.  Herein, S and N co-doped graphene-based nickel cobalt sulfide aerogels are synthesized for use as rechargeable alkaline battery electrodes and oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) catalysts.  Notably, this system shows improved cyclability due to the stabilization effect of the S and N co-doped graphene aerogel (SNGA). Importantly, the results here confirm that the Ni-Co-S ternary materials behave as an electrode for rechargeable alkaline batteries rather than supercapacitors electrodes in three-electrode test as commonly described and accepted in the literature.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanomaterials and Technologies for Lithium-Ion Hybrid Supercapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Haichen; Zhu, Yuan-En; Yang, Jiqian; Wei, Jinping; Zhou, Zhen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemNanoMat</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">For developing energy storage devices with both high energy and power d., lithium-ion hybrid supercapacitors (LIHSs) are the optimal candidate to bridge the gap between lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and conventional supercapacitors.  A LIHS consists of a capacitor-type cathode and a LIB-type anode.  However, the particle size of LIB-type anode materials should be within ≈10 nm to overcome the sluggish ion diffusion in the bulk.  In addn., capacitor-type cathode materials are required to possess high capacitance to match with LIB-type anodes.  Meanwhile, pre-lithiation proves an effective strategy to achieve high cell voltages and high energy d. accordingly.  In this review we first summarize the requirement on electrode materials for LIHSs, and then propose two levels of LIHSs with the clarification of true LIHSs according to the energy and power d.  Finally, we give an outlook for future LIHSs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201702588"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.-E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/C nanofibers prepared through electrospinning with prolonged cycle durability for high-rate sodium–ion batteries induced by pseudocapacitance</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>13</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1702588</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Matrixes for Binder-Free Nanostructured Electrodes in Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Lihan; Qin, Xianying; Zhao, Shiqiang; Wang, Aurelia; Luo, Jun; Wang, Zhong Lin; Kang, Feiyu; Lin, Zhiqun; Li, Baohua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">24</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1908445</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Com. lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), limited by their insufficient reversible capacity, short cyclability, and high cost, are facing ever-growing requirements for further increases in power capability, energy d., lifespan, and flexibility.  The presence of insulating and electrochem. inactive binders in com. LIB electrodes causes uneven active material distribution and poor contact of these materials with substrates, reducing battery performance.  Thus, nanostructured electrodes with binder-free designs are developed and have numerous advantages including large surface area, robust adhesion to substrates, high areal/specific capacity, fast electron/ion transfer, and free space for alleviating vol. expansion, leading to superior battery performance.  Herein, recent progress on different kinds of supporting matrixes including metals, carbonaceous materials, and polymers as well as other substrates for binder-free nanostructured electrodes in LIBs are summarized systematically.  Furthermore, the potential applications of these binder-free nanostructured electrodes in practical full-cell-configuration LIBs, in particular fully flexible/stretchable LIBs, are outlined in detail.  Finally, the future opportunities and challenges for such full-cell LIBs based on binder-free nanostructured electrodes are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703268"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saurel, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Orayech, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carriazo, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">From charge storage mechanism to performance: A roadmap toward high specific energy sodium-ion batteries through carbon anode optimization</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical performance of porous carbon/tin composite anodes for sodium-ion and lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Yunhua; Zhu, Yujie; Liu, Yihang; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">128-133</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.  Understanding of the reaction mechanism between Sn and Na ions will provide insight towards exploring and designing new alloy-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1039/C9TA01999E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Core–shell MOF-derived N-doped yolk–shell carbon nanocages homogenously filled with ZnSe and CoSe<sub>2</sub> nanodots as excellent anode materials for lithium- and sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Core-shell MOF-derived N-doped yolk-shell carbon nanocages homogenously filled with ZnSe and CoSe2 nanodots as excellent anode materials for lithium- and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Xuejiao; Liu, Xiaojie; Chen, Kai; Wang, Gang; Wang, Hui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel and careful design of N-doped yolk-shell carbon nanocage homogenously filled with ZnSe and CoSe2 nanodots derived from core-shell ZIF-8@ZIF-67 was formulated for use as an anode in LIBs/SIBs.  In this unique structure, ZnSe and CoSe2 nanodots were homogeneously distributed in the confined carbon network with hollow interiors.  This kind of complexed structure can offer several attractive features, as follows. (a) The two transition metal selenides, namely, ZnSe and CoSe2, acting as active anodic materials can induce unique semiconducting characteristics and relatively high theor. capacities. (b) The interfacial effect (built-in potential) between ZnSe and CoSe2 can effectively accelerate the diffusion rates of lithium/sodium ions in the solid. (c) The MOF-derived N-doped amorphous carbon with high mesoporosity-acting as a confined reactor-can increase the thermodn. stability, inhibit structural instability, and alleviate unavoidable vol. variations of the active materials.  The hollow interior in a single carbon nanocage could provide a large void space, further buffering the vol. change and stabilizing the structure during charge-discharge.  Inspired by these merits, the as-obtained ZnSe-NC@CoSe2-NC, when used as an anode for LIBs and SIBs, can exhibit superior electrochem. performances with a reversible charge storage capacity of 717.8 mA g-1 after 600 cycles at a c.d. of 0.2 A g-1 in LIBs and 308.5 mA g-1 after 150 cycles at a c.d. of 0.1 A g-1 in SIBs.  Even when tested in full-cell LIBs, the electrochem. results are promising.  These electrochem. results were also confirmed by d. functional theory calcns.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE01377B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lei, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Stringed “tube on cube” nanohybrids as compact cathode matrix for high-loading and lean-electrolyte lithium–sulfur batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stringed "tube on cube" nanohybrids as compact cathode matrix for high-loading and lean-electrolyte lithium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Gaoran; Lei, Wen; Luo, Dan; Deng, Yaping; Deng, Zhiping; Wang, Deli; Yu, Aiping; Chen, Zhongwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The rational design of cathode host materials is significant in fulfilling high-efficiency sulfur electrochem. as well as boosting the energy d. of lithium-sulfur (Li-S) batteries.  Herein, we develop a stringed "tube on cube" nanohybrid (CPZC) with a ternary hierarchical architecture, which contains a fibrous carbon skeleton, highly porous carbon cube filler, and abundant CNT tentacles as an advanced matrix for sulfur electrodes.  The as-developed CPZC delivers excellent cond., abundant active interfaces, and strong confinement to polysulfide, and thus is capable of significantly expediting the sulfur redox kinetics and promoting battery durability.  The fabricated sulfur electrode achieves a superb rate capability up to 10 C, outstanding cyclability over 2000 cycles, and more importantly, excellent performance under high a sulfur loading and sparing electrolyte with a high energy d. of 348.8 W h kg-1 and 327.6 W h L-1 at the system level, which reveals its potential in promoting the practical application of Li-S batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2014.12.074"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical architectured NiS@SiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles enveloped in graphene sheets as anode material for lithium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hierarchical architectured NiS@SiO2 nanoparticles enveloped in graphene sheets as anode material for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Zijia; Zhao, Hailei; Zeng, Zhipeng; Gao, Chunhui; Wang, Jie; Xia, Qing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A well-designed hierarchical architecture NiS@SiO2/graphene was prepd. through electrostatic self-assembly between (3-aminopropyl) triethoxysilane (APTES)-modified NiS and graphene in aq. solns. at room temp.  The obtained composite possesses a unique structure with SiO2 ultrasmall nanoparticles (3-5 nm) derived from the pyrolysis of APTES homogeneously anchored on the surface of NiS nanoparticles (100 nm), forming NiS@SiO2 core-shell hybrid particles, which are well enveloped in graphene sheets.  The SiO2 nanoparticles act as pillars to form open space between graphene sheets and NiS particles, which can buffer the vol. change and afford easy electrolyte-wetting and fast Li ion transport channels.  The graphene sheets can not only significantly enhance the overall elec. cond. of the NiS@SiO2/graphene electrode, but also serve as a blanket to wrap NiS particle and so as to avert its exfoliation from electrode due to large vol. change during cycling.  The prepd.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin (Sn) has wide prospects in applications as an anode electrode material for Li-ion batteries, due to its high theor. specific capacity.  However, the large vol. expansion of Sn during the charge-discharge process causes a performance redn. of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  Here, Sn encapsulated N-doped porous carbon fibers (Sn/NPCFs) were synthesized through an electrospinning method with a pyrolysis process.  This structure was beneficial for the lithium ion/electron diffusion and buffered the large vol. change.  By adjusting the amt. of Sn, the hybrid carbon fibers with different Sn/carbon ratios could be prepd., and the morphol., compn. and properties of the Sn/NPCFs were characterized systematically.  The results indicated that the Sn/NPCFs with a Sn-precursor/polymer wt. ratio at 0.5 : 1 showed the best cycling stability and specific capacity, preserving the specific capacity of 400 mA h g-1 at the c.d. of 500 mA g-1 even after 100 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201901261"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vasileff, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Graphitic carbon nitride (g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>)-derived N-rich graphene with tuneable interlayer distance as a high-rate anode for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly active CoP-Co2N confined in nanocarbon enabling efficient electrocatalytic immobilizing-conversion of polysulfide targeting high-rate lithium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Xiaojun; Gao, Tianqi; Ren, Wenhao; Zhao, Chuan; Liu, Zhi-Hong; Li, Linbo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Energy Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">75</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">250-259</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Science Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-sulfur batteries suffer from poor cycling stability because of the intrinsic shuttling effect of intermediate polysulfides and sluggish reaction kinetics, esp. at high rates and high sulfur loading.  Herein, we report the construction of a CoP-Co2N@N-doped carbon polyhedron uniformly anchored on three-dimensional carbon nanotubes/graphene (CoP-Co2N@NC/CG) scaffold as a sulfur reservoir to achieve the trapping-diffusion-conversion of polysulfides.  Highly active CoP-Co2N shows marvelous catalytic effects by effectively accelerating the redn. of sulfur and the oxidn. of Li2S during the discharging and charging process, resp., while the conductive NC/CG network with massive mesoporous channels ensures fast and continuous long-distance electron/ion transportation.  DFT calcns. demonstrate that the CoP-Co2N with excellent intrinsic cond. serves as job-synergistic immobilizing-conversion sites for polysulfides through the formation of P···Li/N···Li and Co···S bonds.  As a result, the S@CoP-Co2N@NC/CG cathode (sulfur content 1.7 mg cm-2) exhibits a high capacity of 988 mAh g-1 at 2 C after 500 cycles, which is superior to most of the electrochem. performance reported.  Even under high sulfur content (4.3 mg cm-2), it also shows excellent cyclability with high capacity at 1 C.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c07535"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Covalent fixing of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with SnS nanoparticles anchored on g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/graphene boosting fast charge/ion transport for sodium-ion hybrid capacitors</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Covalent Fixing of MoS2 Nanosheets with SnS Nanoparticles Anchored on g-C3N4/Graphene Boosting Fast Charge/Ion Transport for Sodium-Ion Hybrid Capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yuan, Xing; Qiu, Shuting; Zhao, Xiaojun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
